Preface
This thesis is the completion of a master degree in Architectural Engineering at the
Technical University of Denmark, and is the result of 6 months' work equivalent to the
workload of 30 ECTS credits. The project was supervised by Prof. Lisbeth M. Ottosen
and Assoc. Prof. Pernille E.Jensen. I am grateful to both supervisors for advice and
guidance throughout the whole period. The research has been carried out from February
to July 2016 in collaboration with fellow student, Esben Østergård Hansen, who worked
on a similar project in the same time period. The report is written by undersigned but
in some cases gures and results will appear in both reports. When this is the case a
comment is included.
The rst part of the project presents a Life Cycle Assessment and the second part
presents the experimental research. Part one covers about 20 % of the thesis and part
two covers about 80 %.
A special thanks for assistance and guidance during the experiments in the concrete
laboratory is given to technician Per Leth. Without Per the casting of lightweight ag-
gregate concrete would have been close to impossible. Also thanks to the laboratory
technicians Ebba Schnell and Malene Grønvold for helping with experiments regard-
ing investigations carried out in the chemistry lab. Thanks to Weber Saint Gobain for
delivering materials and knowledge of mixing procedure, and a special thanks to Jes-
per Ketelsen who helped with the environmental product declaration and Assoc. Prof.
Morten Birkved for guidance concerning the life cycle assessment.
Last but not least thanks to Esben Østergård Hansen and his everlasting good spirit.

==PAGE:7==
Abstract
Sewage sludge ash can be used in lightweight aggregate concrete as a partial replacement
of cement.
An expanding building industry all over the world combined with an increasing de-
mand for green solutions, lead to a relevant investigation of partial replacements of
Portland cement in concrete. The use of sewage sludge ash (SSA) in standard concrete
has been investigated before with positive results. This thesis will investigate whether
positive results can be obtained for using SSA as a partial replacement for cement in
lightweight aggregate concrete (LWAC) as well.
Part one investigates environmental impacts of using SSA in LWAC. A life cycle
assessment (LCA) is performed for a standard wall constructed with LWAC blocks and
it is concluded that if 45 % of the cement is replaced with SSA, the CO2 emissions
caused by production, transportation, use, and end of life for that wall, could decrease
with a total of 13 %.
The environmental impacts of using SSA in LWAC have also
been investigated by modifying an environmental product declaration (EPD) for 1 ton
of LWAC blocks. It was concluded that if 45 % of the cement is replaced with SSA, the
CO2 emissions caused by production and transportation, could decrease with a total of
22 %.
Part two investigates the practical possibility of replacing a partial amount of cement
in LWAC with SSA.
The sewage sludge ash is obtained from the waste water treatment plant Avedøre and
is taken directly from the production. Characteristics of the ash show that the amount
of the heavy metals Nickel, Lead and especially Cadmium exceed the limits sat by the
Ministry of Environment in the Announcement of sludge, which makes it relevant to
investigate alternative solutions for the use of sewage sludge ash. Investigations of the
water requirement of SSA concluded that the activity factor increases with the percentage
amount of sewage sludge ash in a mortar or concrete mix. This means that when casting
LWAC containing SSA it was necessary to add more water.
==PAGE:8==
iv
The lightweight aggregates (LWA) used in this project are of the sizes 0-2 mm, 2-4
mm and 4-10. Investigation of the water absorption of LWA is crucial when using it as
aggregate in concrete, which is why this was some of the rst investigations completed.
Lightweight aggregate concrete was produced using a block machine consisting of
a mould with a separate lid and an external vibrator.
After lling the mould with
the LWAC mix the vibration was turned on while the compressing force was gradually
increased until reaching 300 kg. After hardening for at least seven days, the blocks were
cut into cylinders with a diameter and height of 75 mm. A total of seven reference blocks
were produced, but as the casting method was not performed in the exact same way for
all blocks only three blocks were considered comparable. A total of seven blocks with a
partial replacement of cement with sewage sludge ash, called SSA-blocks, were produced
in the same way as the comparable Ref-blocks. The SSA-blocks are named: SSA.10,
SSA.15, SSA.20, SSA.25, SSA.30, SSA.35 and SSA.45, where the number refers to the
percentage replacement of cement with SSA. Ref-blocks and SSA-blocks were compared
with LWAC blocks produced in an industrial setting supplied by Weber Saint Gobain.
These blocks are called W-bloks.
Investigations of the LWAC specimens include porosity, density, capillary suction,
leaching of heavy metals and of cause compressive strength. The compressive strength
tests were based on DS/EN 1354.
A leaching test concluded that none of the SSA-blocks exceed the requirements of
category 3 set by the Ministry of Environment in announcement of waste products con-
cerning leaching of heavy metals in construction materials.
According to the compressive strength tests SSA.10 was signicantly weaker than
SSA.15, SSA.20, SSA.25 and SSA.30.
There is no reason to believe that the block
containing the smallest amount SSA should be weaker than the others, therefore the low
strength is ascribed to sources of error during the casting and drilling. From SSA.15 to
SSA.30 the average compressive strengths are more or less the same. When reaching a
replacement of 35 % and 45 % of cement with SSA the strength drops and it was observed
that the blocks were very fragile.
It is concluded that there is no linear correlation
between the replacement of cement with SSA and the compressive strength. However
there is a limit to how much SSA can be added before the structure gets very fragile.
Based on the experiments performed in this thesis it is concluded that up to 30
% of the cement can be replaced with SSA if a strength of approximately 2 MPa is
desired. According to the EPD a replacement of 30 % of the cement will decrease the
global warming potential with 54 kg CO2-eq for 1 ton of lightweight aggregate concrete,
equivalent to 14 %. If the whole life cycle for a standard wall with LWAC is considered,
a 30 % replacement of the cement will decrease the climate change with 47 kg CO2-eq,
equivalent to 9 %.

Chapter 1
Introduction
The world is more than ever aected by climate changes, primarily caused by an increas-
ing emission of CO2. The greatest sinners in this CO2 equation, caused by humans, are
burning of fossil fuels like coal, oil and natural gas, deforestation, and cement production.
The cement production is responsible for about 5 % of the global human caused CO2
emissions. This emission continues to increase because of an expanding building industry
primarily caused by fast growing economies in Asia and other emerging economies.
If the amount of cement in the building industry was lowered, it could have a great
impact on the human caused CO2 emission. Coal y ash (PFA) and silica fume are
by-products of power plants and metal alloying, and they are now used in concrete. PFA
has been an established part of the concrete industry since the 70's [2]. This means that
PFA and silica fume have changed status from waste to resource. Now scientists are
investigating the use of other alternative ashes in concrete, such as sewage sludge ash,
wood ash and cored ash. Earlier studies have showed that sewage sludge ash in some
cases act like a pozzolane which might have an impact on the strength, degree of packing
and water/cement-ratio.
Lightweight aggregates (LWA) are usually produced by a mix of clay with expanding
properties when it is exposed to heat of approx. 1200 ◦C. Burning the clay-mix in a
rotary kiln demands a great amount of energy, which is why it has become common
amongst manufactures of LWA to obtain up to 80 % of the fuel from waste uids such
as paint thinners, used motor oil and spent cleaning uids. Before the energy crisis the
rotary kilns were heated up using only fossil fuels. In the United States about 25 % of
all the produced sewage sludge is used to make lightweight aggregates[18]. All in all the
production of lightweight aggregates is on its way to become very sustainable, but when
producing lightweight aggregate concrete (LWAC) there is still room for improvement.
==PAGE:16==
2
Introduction
The purpose of this thesis is to investigate the possibility of using sewage sludge ash
(SSA) as a replacement of Portland cement in lightweight aggregate concrete. In the rst
part the sustainability of LWAC is investigated with a life cycle assessment (LCA). The
environmental impact of lightweight aggregate concrete is compared with a modied type
of LWAC containing SSA. In the second part the focus is on the practical investigation.
The characteristics of SSA are determined in order to gain knowledge of the contents
of salt and heavy metals. Reference-LWAC is produced and compared with industrially
produced LWAC, then SSA-LWAC is produced and compared with the reference-LWAC.
Apart from gaining knowledge the aim of this project is to nd an upper value for the
use of sewage sludge ash as a cement replacement in lightweight aggregate concrete.
==PAGE:17==
Part I
Life Cycle Assessment
==PAGE:18==
==PAGE:19==
Chapter 2
LCA Introduction
The main reason for investigating SSA as a partial replacement for cement in lightweight
aggregate concrete, is that it is assumed that it will lead to the possibility of creating a
more sustainable version of LWAC. But will it actually have a visible eect, and if so,
how much will the replacement of cement eect the global warming potential (GWP) of
lightweight concrete? To answer these questions a life cycle assessment (LCA) and an
environmental product declaration (EPD) are performed on LWAC blocks.
The LCA is performed using the on-line tool Quantis Suite 2.0 and the environmental
product declaration is performed using an excel-based tool developed by the Danish
Technological Institute based on ISO14025 and EN15804. This tool was provided by
Weber Saint Gobain.
The LCA and the EPD are both performed for the following: REF, SSA.10, SSA.15,
SSA.20, SSA.25, SSA.30, SSA.35, SSA.40, and SSA.45. With the numbers referring to the
amount of replaced cement. In both the LCA and EPD only the potential environmental
impacts will be assessed and the economical and social impacts are not included in the
environmental assessment.
To evaluate the overall sustainability of the product, the
Global Warming Potential (GWP) is used as an indicator.
==PAGE:20==
6
LCA Introduction
2.1
Cement Production and Global Warming
The global climate changes are caused by an increasing concentration of green house
gasses in the atmosphere, primarily carbon dioxide. The emission of CO2 is caused by
both natural and human sources, and even though the emission of human sources are
signicantly smaller than the natural sources, they have increased enough, to upset the
natural balance of the world. [6]
In gure 2.1 the development of global CO2 emissions from fossil-fuel burning, cement
manufacturing, and gas aring are shown in the period 1813-2013. It is very clear that
the CO2 emissions caused by humans took a drastic turn with the beginning of the
industrialisation in the middle of the 19th century.[21]

The demand for cement based materials has increased due to an expanding building in-
dustry all over the world. Therefore the cement production is now responsible for about
5% of the CO2 emission caused by humans, and the industry continues to grow. The
population of the world is growing while the average living standards are getting better,
which means that concrete is used like never before all over the world. The next decade
will see a continuing shift towards faster-growing construction markets in Asia and other
emerging economies where rising populations, rapid urbanisation and strong economic
growth are drivers for construction [17]. In gure 2.2 the increasing cement production
is illustrated by the emission of CO2 caused by the cement production in relation to the

2.2
Lightweight aggregate production and carbon footprints
It has become important for most companies in Denmark to appear with a green prole,
this also accounts for the manufacturers of lightweight aggregate. In the case of Weber,
the LWA factory is already approved as an incineration plant which means that they can
replace over 50% of their energy consumption during the production of LWA by burning
waste from other industries (medical, metal etc.) in stead of fossil fuels. To further
improve their green prole Weber plans to start receiving sewage sludge from the nearby
waste water treatment plants and burn it. This will of course produce sewage sludge ash,
which would be deposited unless they can nd a use for it. If the SSA could be used as
a partial replacement for cement in the production of LWAC blocks, it would not only
benet Weber from an economical point of view, as the purchasing of cement would be
lowered, but it would also improve the green prole of the company. Lowering the use of
cement will have an impact on the environmental product declaration (EPD1), which is
expected to gain greater importance over the next years.

LCA Introduction
==PAGE:23==
Chapter 3
Life Cycle Assessment
The current accepted denition of LCA is: "compilation and evaluation of the inputs,
outputs, and potential environmental impacts of a product system throughout its life cycle"
[19]. The LCA in this project is performed using the online software Quantis Suite 2.0.
This program was chosen because of its simple user interface which allows non-experts
to perform a LCA within a reasonable amount of time.
==PAGE:24==
10
Life Cycle Assessment
3.1
Quantis Suite 2.0
Quantis Suite is a very straight forward LCA tool which can be handled by users without
previous LCA experience. The program has a user friendly interface and is used both for
simple and detailed life cycle assessments. The following description of Quantis Suite is
based on [11].
Quantis Suite operates with the following ve default indicators;
1. Climate change [kg CO2 -eq] (Life Support System)
The global warming Carbon Footprints (CF's) are given for emissions into air and
the used global warming potentials (GWP's) are for a 100-years period.
2. Human Health [DALY]
The unit for human health is: Disability-Adjusted Life Year (DALY). This unit
expresses a human's loss of health caused by exposure to contamination.
The
midpoint categories aecting the human health are human toxicity, respiratory
eects, ionizing radiation, ozone layer depletion and photochemical oxidation.
3. Ecosystem quality [PDF.m2.y]
The unit for ecosystem quality is: Potentially Disappeared Fraction of species on 1
m2 of earth surface during one year. The midpoint categories aecting the ecosys-
tem quality are the aquatic and terrestrial ecotoxicity as well as their acidication,
the aquatic eutrophication, land occupation and water turbined.
4. Resources [MJ]
The midpoint categories with impact on resources are the non-renewable energy
consumption and the extraction of minerals.
5. Water withdrawal [m3]
The inventory of water is the water needed in the whole process whether it is
evaporated, consumed or released again downstream. Drinking water, irrigation
water and water for industrialized processes.
In gure 3.1 an overall scheme of the framework showing the linking between midpoints
and damage categories is shown. It is notable that some midpoint categories eect two
damage categories instead of just one, e.g.
Ozone layer depletion and Photochemical
oxidation. The default indicators in Quantis are equal to the damage categories, except
for Water withdrawal which is categorised as a midpoint category instead of a damage
category.

a total of 130 blocks assembled with 10 mm of mortar. The functional unit in Quantis

3.3
Results
The results are extracted from Quantis Suite and presented in two graphs. In gure 3.6 an
overview of the environmental impact is shown for all nine parts and their corresponding
default indicators. The comparison between the parts is done as a percentage of the
reference. It is evident that within all indicators the impacts decrease when the amount
of cement decreases and is replaced with SSA. The largest percentual dierence is seen
for the human health, which decreases with almost 40 % in the case of SSA.45. This is
partly due to less transportation which aect the ozone layer depletion, human toxicity,
respiratory eects etc.
The water withdrawal is the least changing indicator.
This
corresponds with the fact that SSA requires more water than cement in the mixing
process, so even though it is assumed a free resource, the amount of water added during
manufacturing will increase. The resources decrease to some extend, in the case of SSA.45
it is lowered by 6 %. Considering that the biggest mineral extraction to produce LWC
blocks is the clay which is used for Leca, it is not surprising that lowering the use of
cement only changes the resource indicator slightly. The ecosystem quality, indicated by
the potential disappearance of species on the earth, is decreased by 10.5 % in the case of
SSA.45. This is due to the general lowering of the climate change. The climate change
eect is decreased by 13.5 % in the case of SSA.45.

==PAGE:31==
Chapter 4
Environmental Product
Declaration
An EPD is a method to document and declare the sustainability of a certain product,
and in Denmark it is closely connected to the demands of sustainability set by the
Danish construction products regulation1. One of the reasons that EPDs are useful for a
company to create, is the fact that all LCA tools rely on the EPDs to make a total LCA
of a building. This includes e.g. DGNB2, BREEAM3 and LEED4[1]. An EPD is only
valid if it has been veried by a third party according to ISO 14525, which means that
the EPDs created in this project are not valid. They do, however, give a good indication
of how a replacement of cement with SSA will inuence an EPD.
In this project the EPD is created with an excel-based tool developed by the Danish
Technological Institute based on ISO14025 and EN15804. This tool, including the data
for a standard Leca block 600, was provided by Weber Saint Gobain. The tool considers
the modules from cradle to gate which includes the following:
A1
Raw material supply
A2
Transport
A3
Manufacturing
A4
Distribution
D
Reuse-recovery-recycling-potential
The output of the EPD is arranged in four groups; environmental impacts, resources,
1Byggevareforordningen
2German Sustainable Building Council
3Building Research Establishment's Environmental Assessment
4Leadership in Energy and Environmental Design
==PAGE:32==
18
Environmental Product Declaration
waste and supplementary environmental informations. Just as in the previous chapter,
this project will focus on the environmental impacts. In the EPD these impacts include:
1. Global warming potential: GWP [kg CO2-eq]
Increases in the global mean temperature due to increased amounts of CO2 and
other greenhouse gases in the atmosphere.
2. Ozone layer depletion: COP [kg CFC 11-eq]
Depletion of the ozone layer in the atmosphere due to emission of ozone depleting
substances, such as freon, which has a long life in the atmosphere.
3. Acidication potential: AP [SO2-eq]
A decrease in the pH-value in soil and water systems due to emission of acids/acid-
ifying substances.
4. Eutrophication Potential: EP [kg PO4-eq]
Disturbance of the nutrients balance in soil and water due to increased supply of
nutrients. In water systems an increased algae growth leads to oxygen decits.
5. Photochemical ozone creation potential: POCP [kg ethene-eq]
Ozone creation in the lower parts of the atmosphere. Caused by emission of hydro-
carbons and volatile organic compounds. Inhalation of ozone is harmful to humans
and plants.
6. Depletion of abiotic resources - elements: ADP [kg Sb-eq]
Use of non-renewable resources such as metals and minerals.
Relative amount
specied in relation to the amount of Sb reserves, which is the inorganic element
Antimony.
7. Depletion of abiotic resources - fossil fuels: ADPFossil [MJ (net caloric value)]
Use of fossil resources (oil, coal, gas). Specied as net caloric value at the point
of extraction of the fuel.

declared value 5 is considered to be 1 ton of LWC blocks. As the EPD only considers
from cradle to gate there is no need to look at the LWC blocks in a building context.

A comparison of the climate change from Quantis and the global warming potential from

declared value of the EPD; 1 ton. As mortar represents 0.09 m3 of the 1.3 m3 , and the
average density of a Leca block is 600 kg/m3 the functional unit in Quantis is converted
to 726 kg, which gives a conversion factor of 1.377. This factor is used to scale all the
results from Quantis to a functional unit of 1 ton. The raw data for the graph is listed

the EPD's results, this corresponds to 14.5 %. As seen earlier in gure 3.7 and 4.2 the
impacts from production and transport to costumer are not changing from one scenario
to another. The constant values are given below. The production values are almost the
same in the EPD and Quantis, but the impact due to transportation to costumer is more
than twice as big in Quantis as in the EPD, even though the same distance is used; 200
km. This can be explained by the use of two dierent lorries. In Quantis the chosen
lorry has an impact of 0.108 kg CO2-eq per ton per km, whereas the lorry in the EPD
has an impact of 0.044 kg CO2-eq per ton pr km.

Part II
Experimental Research

Chapter 6
Theory
6.1
SSA
Sewage sludge ash is a by-product of a waste water treatment plant, where sludge from
the sewer undergoes a comprehensive process. First the sludge is cleaned by a mechanical
process, removing elements like hair, faeces and toilet paper. Secondly it undergoes a
biological cleaning where germs convert organic substances into CO2 and bound Nitrogen
into N2. Third and last part of the cleaning concerns the Phosphate caused by detergents.
This is removed chemically by adding Iron Chloride. The Phosphate and the Iron reacts
and create Iron Phosphate which is poorly soluble and settles.
The red color of the
sewage sludge ash is due to the adding of Iron Chloride in the chemical cleaning process.
After these three cleaning processes the sludge is burned and the remaining ash is either
deposited or in some way reused. [3] [4]
Burning process at the waste water treatment plant, Avedøre:
1. A mixture of primary sludge and biologically excess sludge undergoes an anaero-
bically rotting process in huge containers for about 3 weeks. During this process
biogas is produced, which is used for power and heat generation.
2. The sludge is dewatered in centrifuges and is dried until having a dry content of
32%.
3. The sludge is transported to a uid bed oven, in which it is burned at 850 ◦C for
at least two seconds.
==PAGE:42==
28
Theory
4. Flue gas and particles are led to environmental treatment where the ash and par-
ticles are sorted from the ue gas by the electrostatic precipitator.
5. Flue gas is cooled and chemicals are added to bind dioxin and heavy metals, espe-
cially mercury. The remaining particles in the ue gas are captured by bag lters
and led to the ash container.
6. Flue gas is sent to scrubbers where SO2 and HCl are washed out. Afterwards the
gas is heated up to 115 ◦C and is sent out into nature by a 50 m tall chimney.
After the whole burning process the SSA exits the closed system and becomes a pos-
sible danger for the health of the workers. Therefore the ash is wetted to make it easier
to manage when preparing the transportation in containers. The SSA then have a water
content of approx. 20 %.
Table 6.1 contains the demands for PFA when it is used in concrete. These values
should be kept in mind when investigating SSA.
Table 6.1: Chemical demands for PFA, from DS/EN 450-1

6.1.1
Heavy Metals
Sewage sludge ash contain a variety of heavy metals, which is why the ash is normally
placed as landll. Therefore it is very important to know the amount of heavy metals
==PAGE:43==
6.1 SSA
29
in SSA, when it is used in constructions, in order to hinder toxic emissions to the envi-
ronment. The Ministry of Environment in Denmark has established two announcements
that are relevant for using SSA in building materials.
The announcement of sludge1
contain limit values for the content of heavy metals in waste used in a soil-context. The
announcement of waste products2 contain categories for the use of the ash according to
the amount of heavy metals. Category 1 is for all uses in building construction, and
category 2 and 3 are for limited use.

The heavy metals in SSA are usually very volatile, meaning that if the ash gets in
contact with water, the heavy metals are very keen to leach. According to Danish law[16]
the mobility of a substance shall be determined by a batch-leach-test. In some cases the
content of Phosphorous in SSA is investigated because the access to Phosphorous from
primary sources is very limited and it is important to develop methods of recovering
Phosphorous from secondary sources. However this has not been investigated in this
project.
6.1.2
Water Requirement - Activity Factor
In earlier investigations using SSA in concrete one of the conclusions is that the ash
have a higher water requirement than cement, which means that when replacing cement
with sewage sludge ash most likely more water must be added in order to obtain a good
consistency. However it is important not to add to much water because that might result
in a lower w/c-ratio leading to a lower strength. The relation between the water content
and the amount of cement, coal y ash and sewage sludge ash is described with the
1Slambekendtgørelsen [15]
2Bekendtgørelse for restprodukter [16]
==PAGE:44==
30
Theory
equivalent water/cement-ratio.
w/c −eq =
mwater
mcement + 0.5 · mflyash + α · mssa
(6.1)
where α is the activity factor of the sewage sludge ash. Usually the activity factor is a
factor describing the water requirement and the hardening properties, but in this project
the activity factor will only be used as a description of the water requirement for SSA.
6.2
LWA
If a person is in doubt about what a lightweight aggregate is, the author has experienced
that by saying that it is used as sweet soil for house plants, makes everybody familiar with
the small brown and light aggregates. Lightweight aggregates usually have a diameter of
1 - 20 mm and consist of a very porous material with a hard shell, as it can be seen in
gure 6.1. This results in a very light material with a god compressing strength relative
to it's density.
Lightweight aggregates can be divided into two categories; natural aggregates and
industrially produced aggregates. The natural aggregates are primarily volcanic materials
such as lava, volcanic cinders and pomice. The aggregates which are industrially produced
can be divided into two groups:
• Aggregates made by clay, slate and shale clay, which are optimized in the production
of aggregates.
• Aggregates made by waste products, such as y ash or waste cinders, which are
not optimized, but used with their existing properties
Figure 6.1: LWA 4-10mm
==PAGE:45==
6.2 LWA
31
6.2.1
Production
The LWA is produced in a rotary kiln reaching 1100 - 1200 ◦C similar to the ones used
for manufacturing Portland cement. The prepared clay is fed into the higher end of the
kiln, and is gradually transported to the lower end where the ring takes place. Because
of the rotating kiln the clay is formed into round aggregates in varying sizes. The clay
contains organic material which develops gasses when exposed to high temperatures.
These gasses make the clay aggregates expand and because of a relatively quick cooling
procedure in a rotary cooler, the aggregates keep their round shape with thousands of
small air pores inside and a hard ceramic shell. After cooling the aggregates are sieved
into dierent sizes. Unlike cement which is based on feed containing large amounts of
calcium, the LWA are made from materials rich on silica, which release a smaller amount
of CO2. By using a rotary kiln the aggregates end up in dierent sizes and with varying
bulk densities.[18]
(a)
(b)
Figure 6.2: Factory of LWA, Weber Saint Gobain, Hinge, Denmark
The process of producing LWA has been steadily improved in the industry, rst by pre-
liminary treatment of the clay and later by mixing the clay with additives that increased
the size of the bladders, which resulted in lighter aggregates with sucient strength. A
very big step in the technical improvement was the invention of a kiln with two dierent
zones; one for drying and one for burning clay. The rotation velocity in the two zones
are not dependent on each other. Until today Denmark has produced more than 50 mio.
m3 lightweight aggregates [5].
==PAGE:46==
32
Theory
6.2.2
Properties and use
LWA is a versatile material that has various useful properties: the strength-density ra-
tio as mentioned above, a low conductivity coecient granting good thermal insulation
properties, high acoustic resistance, re resistance and indierent towards freezing con-
ditions. Moisture-wise the aggregates have a very quick water suction, and can contain a
lot of water because of the open pore structure, but the capillary eect of a layer of lose
LWA is very low. That is why LWA is used for capillary layers, drainage and ltration in
standard geo-technical solutions. It increases ground stability while reducing settlement
and land deformation.
LWA is of course also used as aggregates in concrete instead of stones, creating a
lighter concrete with less strength.
6.2.3
Water absorption
Because of the very porous structure of lightweight aggregates, it can absorb a lot of water
in a short period of time. In gure 6.3 the absorption interval of Leca 8-20 mm F.11 is
given. The data sheets for the Leca sizes 0-2, 2-4 and 4-10 do not contain absorption
information, but the water absorption for Leca 8-20 will work as a reference value.

6.2.4
A bit of history
The fact that clay expand and produce a kind of bladders when it is exposed to great
heat, was known by producers of bricks, who sometimes could not control the heating of
the kilns. But it was not until 1911 that a brick producer, Stephen Hyde from America,
saw the possibilities of expanded clay and began testing dierent production methods.
In 1918 he got the patent on production of expanding clay aggregates made in a rotary
kiln. The aggregate was called Haydite.3
3Picture is from www.haydite.com
==PAGE:49==
6.3 Concrete
35
Figure 6.5: Haydite production in 1930's
The same kind of tests were made in Denmark, and in 1920 the industrial production
of lightweight aggregates made with plastic clay was patented. In the 1930's the patent
was bought by Lemvigh-Müller & Munck A/S and developed into a patent on expanding
clay in a rotary kiln. The rst kiln in Denmark was established in 1939, which was about
20 years earlier than most countries4.[5]
6.3
Concrete
6.3.1
Standard concrete
To understand LWAC it is preferable to have a general knowledge of standard concrete.
Today all forms of concrete is widely used all over the world either as prefabricated
elements or in-situ.
Concrete has a good durability, good compressing strength and
is re resistant, and compared with other building materials it is cheap. A very basic
concrete consists of cement, sand, stones and water. The modern cement, called Portland
cement, was invented in 1824 and consists of calcium (CaO), Silicon (SiO2), aluminium
(Al2O3) and/or iron (Fe2O3). Cement is categorized as a hydraulic binder, which means
that when it interacts with water it will solidify and harden. One of the most important
4Finland (1950), England (1961), Austria (1961), Germany (1961), Sweden (1962), Norway (1965),
Switzerland (1965), Italy (1966), Belgium (1966) Argentina (1966) Portugal (1970), Algeria (1977), Iran
(1978), Egypt (1979), China (1986), Estonia (1996) and Poland (1999)
==PAGE:50==
36
Theory
factors when mixing a cement paste or a concrete is the water/cement-ratio. A low w/c-
ratio results in a concrete with high strength but low permeability, and on the other
hand, a high w/c-ratio results in lower strength but a high permeability[13][7]. Figure
6.6 shows the coarse structure of cement.
Figure 6.6: SEM of basis cement, zoom x 1500
Admixtures
Admixtures are used in concrete to improve the properties of fresh and hardened concrete,
and they can be divided into sub categories; chemical admixtures, which are used as air
entraining agents and to improve workability, and mineral admixtures which again is
divided into two groups:
• Latent hydraulic waste products, such as coal y ash or silica fume, which have a
pozzolanic eect and are therefore used to improve long term strength and dura-
bility of the concrete.
• Non hydraulic ller materials, like nely ground limestone, which do not have any
pozzolanic eect, but contribute to a better packing of the concrete because of it's
neness.
Pozzolans have been used as a mineral admixture in concrete since the 1950's. They only
have the characteristics of a hydraulic binder when it is exposed to a calcium hydroxide
solution, such as a cement based mortar.[13][7]
==PAGE:51==
6.3 Concrete
37
Coal y ash
Pulverised coal y ash (PFA) is a by-product from coal red power plants and is a
pozzolan. Concrete with coal y ash will get a better long term strength and an improved
workability, which means that the amount of water needed can be reduced. Coal y ash
contains a small amount of unburned carbon, which tend to interact with the surfactants
of the air entraining agents in concrete. This makes the use of air entraining agents
dicult.[2]
Figure 6.7: SEM of Coal y ash, zoom x 1500
6.3.2
Lightweight aggregate concrete
The use of lightweight aggregate concrete (LWAC) can be traced to as early as 3000
BC, where the aggregates were of volcanic origin. With time, the demand for LWAC
increased and technologies were developed to produce the aggregates in factories. The
lightweight aggregates result in a concrete with low density, granting a reduction in
dead load and improving the insulation properties, but of course this also results in a
lower compression strength.
LWAC includes a broad spectre of concrete types, from
no-nes-concrete with low density to structural concrete with densities from 1000 - 2000
kg/m3 with a compressive strength up to 80 MPa. Even though the demand for LWAC
is increasing there is still a lack of adequate explanations concerning the compression
strength and durability of the material. [18]
LWAC is usually pre-casted in a factory because of the diculties of pumping LWAC
in-situ. The pump-pressure forces water into the porous lightweight aggregates resulting
in an increased stiness in the concrete that might block the pipes. The strength of a
lightweight aggregate concrete depends on the strength of the used aggregates. A high-
strength cement-paste will not compensate for low strength aggregates. As the aggregates
==PAGE:52==
38
Theory
absorb a lot of water it is very important to control the water content during a mixing
procedure to prevent the aggregates of stealing water that is assigned to cement. This
is prevented by soaking the aggregates before mixing. The water used for soaking is
ignored when calculating the w/c-ratio. Also the water absorbed by the aggregates may
extend the hydration of the cement and delay and reduce cracking due to drying. The
surface of LWA interacts well with cement compared with stone aggregates, and this
results in minimum of micro-cracking. When seeking the lightest possible LWAC sand
is replaced with lightweight nes, with sizes of 0-2 mm. These very ne aggregates can
cause formations of balls during the mixing procedure. The compaction of the mix is
a consolidation process mostly performed by mechanical vibrators clamped to the form.
[18]
As seen in gure 6.8, the height/diameter-ratio has an inuence on how concrete fail.
Figure 6.8: Failure of dierent H/D-ratios. Figure is taken from [10]
Standard concrete will normally be tested for compression strength with a H/D-ratio
of 2, which results in failure by cracking. When testing LWAC it is assumed that the
failure will be caused by crushing of the lightweight aggregates and not by cracking of
the hardened cement paste, which means that a H/D-ratio ≤1 would be sucient in a
compression test.

average of the results is calculated and the standard deviation, σ, is calculated in excel

using formula 6.7[12]
σ =

(6.7)
where

A one sided T-test will be performed on the compressive strength results to evaluate
the coherence between 14 and 28 days strengths.

Experimental procedures

Experimental procedures
7.1
SSA characterization
In this project two collections of sewage sludge ash have been used. Both collections are
taken directly from Avedøre waste water treatment plant and not from a landll. One
of the samples is collected right after the burning process, described in page 27, and
therefore has close to no water content. The second sample is collected after the wetting.
The two samples are called SSA-1 and SSA-2.
SSA-1
Avedøre
Dry condition. Collected after the burning process in
the uid bed oven.
SSA-2
Avedøre
Moist condition. Collected at the nishing point, after
wetting.
7.1.1
Water content
The water content of the two SSA collections was investigated by weighing three samples
of each collection before and after 24 hours in an oven with 105 ◦C.
7.1.2
Water solubility
SSA and distilled water is mixed, shaken and sat on a table to settle. Little by little the
liquid is poured through a lter which collects the non soluble particles. After repeating
the procedure at least three times the lter is placed on a petri dish in an oven at 105 ◦C,
over night. The following day the dry ash is weighed and the amount of soluble materials
is determined. See appendix D.2 for a more detailed description.
7.1.3
Loss on ignition
The content of organic material in a substance like SSA is more or less equivalent to the
loss on ignition at 550 ◦C. The procedure is further described in appendix D.3
==PAGE:57==
7.1 SSA characterization
43
7.1.4
Solubilisation - ICP
The content of acid soluble metals in the SSA is measured by using an ICP on samples
with a liquid/solid-ratio=2. A thorough description of the process is found in appendix
D.4.
7.1.5
Water requirement
In order to compare the water requirement of SSA with that of cement, various methods
were investigated to determine consistency. The goal was to obtain a reference value
with a cement paste or mortar and then replace relevant percentages of the cement with
SSA and aim after that reference value. This would in the end result in a factor called
the activity factor describing the water requirement of SSA relative to that of cement.
As illustrated in gure 7.1 two apparatuses were used; vicat and ow table. For each
apparatus three dierent methods were performed, four of the methods were according
to a standard and two of the methods were self-developed.

7.2.2

The absorption is essential to investigate in order to gain knowledge of the water re-
quirement when casting lightweight concrete blocks. The absorption and density of the
lightweight aggregates are found using the reference method from DS/EN 1097-6, in
which pycnometers are used.

fraction of LWA (0-2 mm), three small pycnometers, shown in gure 7.5b, were used and
weighed on a scale with four decimal places, shown in gure 7.5c. For the larger fractions
(2-4 mm and 4-10 mm) two big pycnometers, shown in gure 7.5a were used and weighed
on a scale with two decimal places. The mass of the samples used for pycnometer tests

The lightweight aggregates were prepared by drying in an oven until constant mass
was reached. The test portions were immersed in distilled and air free water in the pyc-
nometers. Any entrapped air was removed by gently rolling and jolting the pycnometer
in a tipped position. To keep a constant temperature of the water in the pycnometer and
in order to measure the temperature, it was placed in a water bath with room tempera-
ture. Every time a measurement was needed the pycnometer was opened, overlled with
water, closed again and dried on the outside before it was weighed. When all measure-
ments were complete after 24 hours, the aggregates were removed from the water and
dried to a saturated surface dried condition (ssd) in order to calculate the ssd density.
The fractions 2-4 and 4-10 were dried using dry cloths and the fraction 0-2 was dried
using a warm gentle current of air, using a hair-dryer. To test if the aggregates from
0-2 have reached a ssd-condition, a metal cone mould is used. The aggregates are lled
into the mould and then tapped 25 times with a metal tamper and then the mould is
gently lifted. If the aggregate cone does not collapse, as shown in gure 7.6a, the drying
should continue and the cone test should be repeated until collapse. If the aggregate
cone collapse and form a pyramid with a denite peak and angular slopes, as illustrated
in gure 7.6b, the aggregates are considered to have reached the saturated surface dry

First LWA 0-2 was mixed with 6.4 kg water for a few minutes, then the LWA 2-4 and
4-10 were added. The LWA had one hour to absorb water before cement, coal y ash and
SSA were added. When the binders were added a zero time was noted. In the case of
adding SSA, the additional water corresponding to table 7.3 was added after the binders.
During the whole mixing process LWA 0-2 was very keen to form lumps, sometimes up
to the size of a st. These lumps were crushed to obtain a regular distribution of LWA
in the mixture. The mixture was ready when the consistency made it possible to form a
snowball.
7.3.2
Casting of LWAC blocks
General concept of the block machine
The block machine is shown in gure 7.9. It is a machine which makes it possible to
decrease the size of the mould while under vibrations, causing the mixture to pack as
much as possible.
(a)
With bars and timber
(b)
Without bars and timber
Figure 7.9: Block machine
A Base plate covered with plastic
B Block machine
C Build-in balance measuring the force of compression in kg
D Removable lifting bars resting on timber, used to lift the machine with a fork lift
==PAGE:66==
52
Experimental procedures
The mould consists of three separate parts; the base plate, which makes up the actual
bottom of the mould, the sides, which are a xed part of the machine, and the lid, which
is a movable part, also called the pressure plate. The base plate and the block machine
should be levelled and maintain a gap of 5 mm between the two in order not to transfer
vibrations from the machine to the base plate, once the machine is turned on.
The block machine used in this project is delivered by Abeo, who uses LWAC in SL-
decks which explains the shape of the mould as seen in gure 7.10
Figure 7.10: Casting mould for LWAC blocks. This gure will appear in the thesis of EØH as well.
In this project the shape of the mould is not very relevant because the blocks are cut
into several test specimens after hardening.
Optimal Casting Method
The process of nding the optimal method for casting LWAC blocks using the available
machine is described by gure 7.11.
==PAGE:67==

Figure 7.11: Finding the optimal casting method
It was discovered that if the mould was lled to the top it was virtually impossible
to lift the machine without breaking of the top of the newly casted block. This issue is
illustrated in gure 7.10. A suitable degree of lling was determined to 3 cm from the
top.
Regarding the duration of the vibration some dierent time slabs were tried, but in
the end it was concluded that the actual duration did not matter. It was more important
that the block was compressed as much as possible. In average the vibration endured for
1 - 2 minutes.
The possibility of applying exactly 300 kg instantly was tested. This made it very
dicult to ensure that the lid of the mould was maintained levelled. It was concluded
that the 300 kg should be applied gradually to make a manual levelling of the lid possible.
Blocks made industrially by Weber are compacted 15 % from the original mould size
to the nal size. This was however not possible with the machine available, which is why
the goal was to compress the mixture simply as much as possible.
==PAGE:68==
54
Experimental procedures
Casting Procedure
The lling of the mould should begin no later than 15 minutes after zero time.
• The mould was gradually lled with LWAC mix until 3 cm from the top.
• The pressure plate, also called the lid of the mould, was placed on top of the LWAC
mix.
• A bubble level was placed on top of the pressure plate and a hammer was used to
get it levelled.
• The vibration was turned on and the pressure was gradually increased, while mak-
ing sure that the pressure plate was levelled, and that the lever was square to the
machine.
• Once the pressure reached 300 kg and the pressure plate was level and not moving,
the vibration could be turned o, and the pressure plate lifted.
• The whole machine was lifted using a forklift, as slowly and vertically as possible.
Any sudden movements would damage the block. An example of a damaged block
is shown in gure 7.12b.
The nal result is shown in gure 7.12a.
After moving the machine the block is
wrapped in plastic and left on the spot for 24 hours before it is moved to a controlled
climate.

Standard LWAC block
(b)
SSA.35
Figure 7.12: LWAC blocks immediately after casting

7.3 Lightweight Aggregate Concrete Blocks

7.3.3
Drilling and Cutting Specimens out of Blocks
According to DS/EN 1354 the test specimens shall be cubes or cores with a length equal
to the diameter not deviating more than 5 %. If the test specimens have a diameter of 70
mm or larger, a test set shall consist of at least three specimens. The preferred diameter
of a specimen is 100 mm, but due to the size of the blocks, a diameter of 75 mm was
chosen. This results in a maximum of 12 cores from each block, and 5 cores from a Weber
block. Afterwards most cores could be cut in half granting a total of 24 specimens for
a regular block and 10 specimens for a Weber block. With a diameter of 100 mm only
seven specimens could be cut from each block and additionally only 4 specimens from
a Weber block. Furthermore as the width of a Weber block is exactly 100 mm, it was
practically impossible to drill suitable cores with a diameter of 100 mm. The cutting of
specimens took place 4-7 days before the rst compression test.
Procedure
The drilling of cores was performed using the machine in gure 7.13a. When cutting the
large cores in half the circular saw, seen in gure 7.13b was used. In appendix F.1 a
guide on how to use the drill is found. In the case of SSA-35 and SSA-45 the blocks were
very weak and did not withstand the drilling machine very well. SSA-35 only resulted
in four core-specimens and it was decided to cut the SSA-45 block into cubes in stead of
cylinders. Because of the height of the block, the regular circle saw could not be used,
and in stead the big saw was used, see gure 7.13c.

7.3.4
Compressive strength
Compression tests were performed according to DS/EN 1354-2005. The used apparatus
are listed below:
==PAGE:72==
58
Experimental procedures
• Instron 5500R
• Low density breboards, thickness of 12 ± 2 mm
• Balance with two decimals
• Calliper
• Ventilated drying oven maintaining 105 ◦C.
Compressive strength is dened as the ratio between the rupture load in axial com-
pression and the cross-sectional area of the test specimen.
The load was applied as
extension per time: 0.1 mm per minute. This was chosen in order to apply the load in a
continuous and uniform manner. The possibility of applying the compression as load per
time was opted out to avoid an accelerating extension immediately after rupture, causing
the specimen to shatter.
After rupture the test specimen or at least 80 % of its mass was dried at 105 ◦C until
constant mass, usually obtained within three days. See gure 7.20b. This was done in
order to obtain the water content at the time of compression test.
(a)
Compression test
(b)
Drying specimens after test
Figure 7.17
Fibreboards are used as equalizing layers between the loadbearing surfaces of the
specimen and the platens of the testing machine. A new set of breboards are used
==PAGE:73==
7.3 Lightweight Aggregate Concrete Blocks
59
for each specimen. Figure 7.18 shows how the breboards adapts to the surface of the
specimen and thereby result in a more uniform pressure utilizing a greater surface area.
The breboards are also a good indicator of how big the area of the specimen actually
was.
As seen in gure 7.18 there is a huge dierence in the area of SSA.10.6b and
SSA.10.4a.
Figure 7.18: Fibreboards, 8 x 8 cm, revealing the dierence of the compression area of specimens
7.3.5
Leaching of LWAC
The specimens were milled, mixed with distilled water (liquid/solid-ratio=2) and placed
on a shaking platform for 24 hours. After removing the specimens from the shaking
platform they settled for 15 min, and then the pH-value was measured. Finally the mix
was ltered and the liquid was collected in two bottles, which were sent to ICP.
Batch-leach-tests were performed on the LWAC specimens listed below:
• W.8
• Ref.6.11b
• Ref.7.2b
• SSA.10.8b
• SSA.15.4b
• SSA.20.12a
• SSA.25.12
• SSA.30.4a
• SSA.45.1
==PAGE:74==
60
Experimental procedures
7.3.6
Capillary suction
Test specimens were dried to constant mass and measured in order to calculate the
volume. Several brass bars were placed in a tray and the tray was lled with distilled
water until the water edge reached 5 mm above the brass bars. The test set-up can be
seen in gure 7.19. A zero time was noted when a specimen was placed on the bars
and got in contact with the water. When a measurement was needed the specimen in
question was dried to a surface dry condition using a dish towel, weighed, and replaced
in the tray. Measurements were performed 1, 2, 4, 8, 16, 32, 60, 120, 240, 360 minutes
after zero time, and for the SSA-samples also 24 and 30 hours after zero time. During
the whole test it was assured that the water always reached the bottom 5 mm of the
specimens. The result is given as the absorbed water per absorption area.
Figure 7.19: Capillary suction test
Capillary suction tests were performed on the specimens listed below:
• W.2 (1,2,3)
• Ref.3 (6,11)
• Ref.4 (10,11)
• Ref.5 (6,11)
• Ref.6 (6a, 6b, 11a, 11b)
• Ref.7 (3a, 5a, 8a)
• SSA.10 (1b, 6a, 12b)
• SSA.15 (3b, 7b, 10a)
• SSA.20 (1b, 6a, 12b)
• SSA.25 (6, 11a, 11b)
• SSA.30 (6a, 7a, 10a)
The results are presented as the mass of water [kg] absorbed per cross section area
[m2] as a function of the square root of time [√sec].
==PAGE:75==
7.3 Lightweight Aggregate Concrete Blocks
61
7.3.7
Porosity and Density
The investigation of porosity and density of the LWAC specimens was performed with
a vacuum pumb, a desiccator, seen in gure 7.20a, and a balance capable of weighing
under water, seen in gure 7.20.
The specimens were dried at 105◦C until constant
mass was reached and the dry-mass, mdry was recorded. The specimens were placed in
the desiccator which was connected to the vacuum pump. The pump ran for 3 hours
creating a vacuum, and afterwards distilled water was led into the desiccator covering all
the specimens. The rst hour was passed in vacuum and then the valve of the desiccator
was opened and the specimens stayed under water at atmospheric pressure over night.
The following day the specimens are weighed under water, mw, and in air both including
as much water as possible, ms, and after drying the surface and getting as much as water
from the air voids out, mssd.
(a)
One out of three Desiccators
(b)
Weighing specimens under water
Figure 7.20
The following equations are used to calculate the volume, V, the volume of open pores,
Vp, the dry density (water method), ρdry, and the porosity, P.
V [m3] = ms −mw
ρw
,
ρdry[kg/m3] = mdry
V
(7.1)
Vp[m3] = ms −mdry
ρw
,
P[%] = Vp
V · 100
(7.2)

8.1.4
SEM pictures of SSA
Figure 8.1 shows SEM pictures of the sewage sludge ash from Avedøre, raw and milled,

investigated earlier [14].

• TI-B 18 (86) which determines the consistency of a cement paste
• DS/EN 450 which determines the water requirement of a coal y ash in mortar

Figure 8.6 shows the SEM pictures of the three sizes of LWA. Figure 8.6a and 8.6d illus-

of the aggregates is thin, but consistent.

8.2.3
Density of LWA
The apparent particle density, the oven-dried density and the ssd-density is calculated
for LWA 0-2, 2-4 and 4-10 using the formulas described chapter 27. The bulk density
is calculated based on all the measurements of the dry aggregates used for mixing. See
appendix F.4.
Figure 8.8 contains the values of the dierent densities. Not surprisingly the following
correlation is seen between the densities:
==PAGE:85==
8.2 LWA
71
ρ0−2 > ρ2−4 > ρ4−10
This corresponds with the SEM-pictures and means that LWA 2-4 most likely has a
larger compression strength than LWA 4-10. When comparing the dierent densities for
LWA 2-4 and 4-10 the following correlation is seen:
ρssd > ρapparent > ρoven−dry > ρbulk
This however does not completely apply for LWA 0-2 as it is seen that the bulk
density is signicantly lower than the other densities, but the oven-dried density, apparent
particle density and ssd-density are practically the same. This implies that LWA is not
very porous.

8.3
LWAC
The following section will consider three types of LWAC blocks:
W-blocks
are industrially produced LWAC blocks (Leca Block 600 from Weber
Saint Gobain)
Ref-blocks
have a mix identical to W-blocks, but they are casted as described in
appendix F.1.
SSA-blocks
are almost identical to Ref-blocks, but a certain amount of cement is
replaced with SSA.
8.3.1
Water content in LWAC blocks
Calculations are found in appendix F.3.
The water assigned to cement and coal y ash is obtained from the mixing-ratios given
by the supplier of W-blocks. The mass of water assigned to binder is 2.56 kg (see table
7.2). By using equation 6.1 for a reference block the w/c-eq is found.
w/c −eq =
2.56kg
(2.82kg + 0.5 · 1.15kg) = 0.754
(8.1)
The absorption of LWA is very important when designing the mix of LWAC. As
described in the mixing process it was decided to mix LWA and water rst and letting
the aggregates absorb water for one hour. Therefore the average water absorption of
the aggregates after one hour is calculated and used when scaling the mixing-ratios.
Table 8.5 contains the average absorption values for for the LWA after one hour and the
corresponding amount of soaking water for a reference LWAC block. The water assigned
to LWA and the water assigned to binder are the calculated minimum amount of water
for a block. However it was experienced that to obtain a consistency which made it
possible to form a snowball, more water was needed. Through an iterative process for a
Ref-block an acceptable amount of extra water was estimated to: 1.64 kg. It is expected
that the extra water interacts with the aggregates and not the cement and coal y ash,
meaning that the w/c-eq stays the same.

8.3.2
Density and Porosity
Figure 8.9 illustrates the dry density of the all the blocks, except SSA.35 and SSA.45, as
they did not provide enough specimens. Raw data is found in appendix F.9. Two methods
of measuring the dry density was performed. The water method is described in section
7.3.7 and the geometric method is based on the mass of a dry specimen in relation to
==PAGE:88==
74
Results and discussion
the measured volume. The density of the W-block is around 675 kg/m3, which is higher
than the prescribed density of 600±50 kg/m3. When drilling cores and cutting of edges,
it was experienced that sometimes dust from the cutting mixed with water, lled the
air voids in the blocks. Despite attempts to remove the dust, some of it might still be
trapped inside the specimens causing a higher density. The reference samples vary from
600 to 700 kg/m3 which is quite a deviation, but as Ref.5 was compressed with an instant
application of load, instead of gradually applying the load, this might have inuenced
the packing of the aggregates in another way, causing a dierent structure with lower
density. In most cases the geometric method result in a lower density of the specimens
than the water method. This might be caused by a measured volume that is bigger than
the actual one, because the volume is based on average of three diameters and the height.
If the specimens were not a perfect cylinder the measured volume would be bigger than
the actual one. All SSA-blocks have an average density within 625 to 680 kg/m3. SSA.25
have a very large standard deviation in the density which gives reason to believe that
the structure of the block is uneven and that something during the casting process has
been dierent.

8.3.4
Compression tests
Raw data for all compression tests are found in appendix F.7.
The W-blocks have been tested under dierent conditions in order to investigate the
methods of testing. As seen in gure 8.13 some blocks were tested with a height-diameter-
ratio of 1, as prescribed in [9], and others were tested with a H/D-ratio of 2, as prescribed
in [8]. There has also been a comparison of two kinds of breboards, called hard and
soft, and the appearance of the surface; raw vs. cut. A comparison of samples with a
diameter of 100 mm and 75 mm was also conducted. The comparison is based on the
compression strength and the water content at the test moment is measured and written
(in black) in the bars. The standard deviation is illustrated with error-bars.
==PAGE:91==
8.3 LWAC
77
It is evident that the specimens tested with a raw surface (W.1) have a big standard
deviation compared to the rest and it is also the third lowest in average strength. When a
soft breboard is pressed against a rough and irregular surface it will shape appropriate
to the surface of the specimen and therefore the load will spread over a greater cross
area.
The W-specimens tested with soft breboards have a compression strength of
approximately 3.0 MPa. The hard breboard on the other hand will not change its shape
and the load will work on a smaller area that might cause the specimen to rupture at
lower load. W.5 and W.7 are tested with hard breboards and it is noticed that they are
the ones with lowest average strength of all seven W-blocks, furthermore W.5 has a huge
standard deviation. It seems that there is no denite dierence of the strength depending
on the H/D-ratio. This corresponds with the theory prescribing that the weakest part
of the LWAC is the LWA, that break by crushing. Ordinary concrete normally ruptures
due to cracks in the hardened cement paste, which make the H/D-ratio very decisive in
that case.

There is a very big variation in the compression strength of the references, in the
following some explanations of why, is given, and it is stated which Ref-blocks are used
to calculate an average reference value.
Ref.1 Tested after 50 days. The mix of Ref.1 contain 770 g more water than the rest of
the Ref-blocks and the compression during the vibration did not reach more than
==PAGE:93==
8.3 LWAC
79
250 kg. This resulted in a dense structure with high density. As stated earlier, a
high density increases the strength. Ref.1 is not included in the average.
Ref.2 Tested after 49 days. Only two specimens were tested so according to [9] it is not
statistically approved. The compression during the vibration did not reach 250 kg.
Ref.1 is not included in the average.
Ref.3 Tested after 49 days of hardening but apart from that the block was produced and
treated correctly. Ref.3 is included in the average.
Ref.4 Tested after 30 days. As stated in table F.1 the compression during the vibration
was applied very instant with the exact weight of 300 kg. By applying the com-
pression very instant it has not given the aggregates time to pack, and the result is
a bad structure of the block with little cohesiveness. This results in a low strength.
Ref.4 is not included in the average.
Ref.5 Tested after 16 and 28 days. This block was also compressed with instantly applied
force of 300 kg, causing a bad packing of the concrete and a low strength. Ref.5 is
not included in the average.
Ref.6 Tested after 14 and 28 days. The water content of the 28-days specimens is almost
to low (4% is the limit [9]) and the standard deviation of the specimens is also
very big, but the procedure of casting was as prescribed. Ref.6 is included in the
average.
Ref.7 Tested after 14 and 28 days with an acceptable water content. The procedure of
casting was as prescribed. Ref.7 is included in the average.

SSA.10 The 14 days specimens have an average strength that is 0.15 MPa bigger than that
of 28 days samples. The standard deviations are in both cases 0.2 MPa. It was not
expected that the block containing the least SSA, and therefore most cement, would
be the weakest of the SSA-blocks. There is reason to believe that an irregularity
has happened during the casting or drilling process. It was noted during the testing
that some of the specimens had a very small surface area as seen in gure 7.18, in
these cases it has not been possible to establish the exact surface area exposed to
compression test. This will inuence the compression value when calculating from
kN to MPa.
SSA.15 The 14 days specimens have an average strength that is 0.19 MPa bigger than that
of 28 days samples. The standard deviations are in both cases 0.3 MPa.
SSA.20 The 28 days specimens have an average strength that is 0,23 MPa bigger than that
of 14 days samples. The standard deviations are in both cases 0.2 MPa.
SSA.25 The average strength of the 28 days specimens is 0.19 MPa larger than that of 14
days, but the observations have a standard deviation of 1.0 Mpa, which is very big.
This corresponds with the very big deviation within the densities, as seen in gure
8.9. The water content of the 28 days specimens is lower than the limit of 4 %.
SSA.30 When comparing the average strength of 14 and 28 days specimens there is almost
no dierence, even the deviations are close to the same. The water content of the
28 days specimens is lower than the limit of 4 %.
==PAGE:95==
8.3 LWAC
81
SSA.35 SSA.35 was damaged greatly during the casting, when lifting the block machine as
seen in gure 7.12b. This might have damaged the coherence of the whole block.
The weakness was noticed already when cutting cores. The block practically broke
under the shear forces of the drill and this resulted in only four specimens with
very pour shapes, seen in gure 7.14a. The low strength might be because of a
broken structure within the cement paste and/or because of the reduced load area.
Because of the small amount of specimens it was decided only to perform 28-days
tests. The water content was lower than 4 %.
SSA.45 When casting SSA.45 everything seemed normal, but when the time came for cut-
ting cores it quickly became clear that the shear forces were to great. The block
simply cracked inside the drill resulting in an alternative cutting of the block into
cubes, as seen in gure 7.14b, however it did still only result in 4 specimens. Be-
cause of the small amount of specimens it was decided only to perform 28-days
tests. The water content was lower than 4 %.
Comparing 14 days strength with 28 days strength
When comparing the average compressive strengths for 14 and 28 days respectively, it
seems that there is no signicant increasing in strength after 28 days. To investigate this
a standard T-test was performed for each block, comparing the compressive strength
after 14 and 28 days respectively. The test was performed at a 5 % signicance level for
all the blocks with both 14 and 28 days strengths: Ref.6, Ref.7, SSA10, SSA.15, SSA.20,
SSA.25 and SSA.30. The calculations were performed using analysis tool in Excel and
the outputs are given in appendix F.7.5.
Ref.6 and SSA.20 showed a signicantly higher 28 days strength (P=0.046 and P=0.021).
Ref.7 showed a signicantly higher 14 days strength (P=0.047). For the remaining blocks
there were no signicant dierences between the strength for 14 and 28 days (P>0.05).
In the case of standard concrete there is no doubt that the 28 days strength would
be higher than for 14 days, but in the case of LWAC it is reasonable that the dierence
of 14 and 28 days of hardening does not aect the compression strength.
Once the
cement paste has hardened enough to reach the strength of the lightweight aggregates,
the weakest part of the mix would be the aggregates, and additional hardening time would
not necessarily result in an increased compression strength. It has not been possible to
obtain any literature describing the strength development over time of a lightweight
aggregate concrete, but the following citation is from [18]1 "A very high-strength cement
paste does not compensate for a lightweight aggregate of low density and strength.". Based
on the outcome of the T-test and a discussion of the plausibility, it was assessed that it
is reasonable to combine the strengths of 14 days and 28 days within each block.

Figure 8.16 compares the average compression strength of all blocks (14 and 28 days
combined). It is evident that the Ref-specimens have a higher strength than the SSA-
specimens and also a bigger standard deviation, which is partially explained by the
fact that the average is based on 3 Ref-blocks, creating a risk of deviations within the
mixing and casting procedure, the storing conditions and/or the water content during
testing. When looking at SSA.15 - SSA.30 there is no signicant tendency following the
replacement of cement with SSA. This conrms that the compression strength of the
blocks are dependant on the strength of the aggregates rather than the exact amount of
cement. The reduction of cement does however aect the coherence of the block, and
when replacing above 30 % of the cement with SSA the coherence was decreased which
was noticed during the drilling and cutting of SSA.35 and SSA.45. This indicates that
the upper limit for the replacement of cement with SSA is between 30 % and 35 %.
If the using of sewage sludge ash as a partial replacement of cement is to be im-
plemented in an industrialized setting, an indication of the strength is estimated by
multiplying the average strengths of SSA-blocks with the W/Ref-ratio. The results are
listed in table 8.9.

Leaching
Figure 8.17 shows the results of leaching tests performed on all blocks and compared with
the criteria of the announcement on waste products [16]. Raw data is found in appendix
F.10. All blocks stay within category 3 for all substances. In the case of Cadmium,
Copper, Manganese and Zinc all samples stay within category one and two. Though it is
evident that Ref-blocks and SSA-blocks leach more Zinc than Weber it is still not more
than the limit for category one and two. In the case of Arsenic, Lead and Chromium all
samples stay within category 3. It seems like an increasing of Chromium when adding
SSA to the blocks, but it is still within the limits. When looking at Barium it is clear
that the blocks containing SSA leach more Barium, but it is strange that the reference
leaches the most and that it seems like the more SSA, the less leaching of Barium. The
leaching of Sodium increases compared with the W-block, but the amount is still within

8.4
Sources of error
This section is written in collaboration with EØH.
Though calculations were carried out to estimate the amount of water required for
the LWA and bio ash, it became clear when mixing the concrete that extra water had to
be added in order to obtain a desirable consistency. It is assumed that the extra water
does not change the w/ceq, but it is impossible to know for sure.
Lifting the block machine after creating a Lightweight aggregate concrete block was
not always conducted as vertically as intended, mainly because of it being dicult to
steer a forklift gently and precise. In various cases the top of the block was damaged
==PAGE:99==
8.4 Sources of error
85
and had to be cast aside, but the remaining coherent block was used even though the
structure might have been damaged during the lifting of the machine.
After drilling and cutting cylinders some specimens were partially lled with a mixture
of dust and water from the process. The dust and water mix was removed as much as
possible, but it is plausible that some was left causing an increasing of the mass.
DS/EN 1354 states that the water content of a specimen should not be lower than
4 % when tested for compressive strength. However, this was the case in several cases
of the 28-days strength tests. Though the test results do not give reason to believe it,
the low water content might have aected the compressive strength. DS/EN 1354 also
states that a block should be cut into smaller specimens, at the earliest, 7 days before the
testing date. As the cylinders for testing both 14 and 28 days strengths were cut from
the same block, on the same date, all the specimens for 28 days strengths have been cut
too early.
Some blocks turned out to have poor resistance when drilling cylinders, causing edges
to break of the specimens. This made the measuring of the volume for some specimens
very cumbersome leading to imprecise densities and perhaps a misleading compressive
strength.
==PAGE:100==
86
Results and discussion
==PAGE:101==
Chapter 9
Conclusion
Characteristics of sewage sludge ash show that the ash does not meet the requirements
set by the Ministry of Environment in the Announcement of sludge. The amount of
Nickel, Lead and especially Cadmium exceeds the limits. This means that the ash must
be deposited unless an alternative use could be found, as investigated in this project.
Investigation of the water requirement of SSA led to the conclusion that the activity factor
increases when the percentual amount of SSA increases in the LWAC mix. Approximate
values of the activity factor were based on the performed tests, and used to determine the
approximate water requirement of sewage sludge ash when used in lightweight aggregate
concrete.
An investigation of lightweight aggregates conclude that the aggregate fraction 0-2
mm is in fact larger aggregates that have been crushed and not small round aggregates.
The size 2-4 mm and 4-10 mm are round porous structures with a hard ceramic shell and
a bulk density of respectively 365 kg/m3 and 304 kg/m3. The densities of the aggregates
decrease when the the size of the aggregates increase. The aggregates with diameter of
2-10 mm absorb water very quickly. After one hour they have absorbed about 24 - 34 %
of their own dry weight and afterwards the absorption curve levels.
An approximation of the amount of water added to the LWAC-mix was based on the
approximate water requirement of SSA and the water absorption of LWA. To obtain an
acceptable consistency of the mix, more water was added during an iterative process.
Five out of seven blocks needed additional water, in one case 26 % extra water was
added. The measured dry density of W-blocks is 675 kg/m3. The dry densities of the
Ref- and SSA-blocks were between 600 kg/m3 and 700 kg/m3 and there was no evident
correlation between the density and the amount of SSA. The porosities of the Ref- and
SSA-blocks were between 55 vol-% and 65 vol-%, which was very close to the W-blocks
==PAGE:102==
88
Conclusion
with a porosity of 66 vol-%.
Based on background knowledge of lightweight aggregate concrete supplemented by
statistical calculations it was concluded that a combination of 14 and 28 days compres-
sive strength data is reasonable. Therefore averages were calculated across the age of
specimens when tested. The compression test method prescribed in DS/EN 1354 was
accepted due to the ability of obtaining an average strength of W-blocks of 3.03 MPa
with a deviation of 0.33 MPa, corresponding to the prescribed value from the supplier.
The obtained average compression strength of the Ref-blocks was 2.17 MPa with a stan-
dard deviation of 0.62 MPa. The dierence in strength from W-block to Ref-blocks is
ascribed to dierences in the casting procedure, mainly due to the available machinery.
The W/Ref-ratio is 1.40. The SSA-blocks are generally weaker than the Ref-blocks, but
it seems that there is no linear tendency between the replacement of cement with SSA
and the compressive strength. If SSA-blocks are to be produced in an industrial setting,
it is assumed that the strengths will correspond to the averages obtained in this project
multiplied by the W/Ref-ratio. This results in the following strengths:
SSA.10
1.7 MPa
SSA.15
2.4 MPa
SSA.20
2.0 MPa
SSA.25
2.2 MPa
SSA.30
2.1 MPa
SSA.35
1.0 MPa
SSA.45
1.6 MPa
The weakness of SSA.10 is ascribed to dierences in the casting and drilling. SSA.15
- SSA.30 maintain more or less the same average strength. When reaching a replacement
of 35 % and 45 % of cement with SSA the strength drops and it was observed that the
blocks were very fragile. A leaching test concluded that the SSA-blocks do not exceed
the requirements of category 3 set by the Ministry of Environment in announcement of
waste products concerning leaching of heavy metals in construction materials.
Based on the experiments performed in this thesis it is concluded that up to 30 % of
the cement can be replaced with SSA if a strength of approximately 2 MPa is desired.
According to the EPD a replacement of 30 % of the cement will decrease the global
warming potential with 54 kg CO2-eq for 1 ton of lightweight aggregate concrete. If the
whole life cycle for a standard wall with LWAC is considered, a 30 % replacement of the
cement will decrease the climate change with 47 kg CO2-eq.
==PAGE:103==
9.1 Further Investigations
89
9.1
Further Investigations
This section was written in collaboration with EØH.
• Ideally, the next step is upscale testing, in an industrial setting.
It would be
interesting to see, if the same results could be repeated.
• Verication of activity factor, by testing on concrete mix instead of mortar.
• It was seen that the compressive strength was more or less the same for 14- and 28-
day tests, which would imply that the nal strength was reached sometime before
the 14-day mark. It would be interesting to see, at what point within the 14 days
the strength is reached, and what eect the ash has on this.
• Around 30 % of the binder in the investigated lightweight aggregate concrete is coal
y ash. Replacing some of this with a bio ash, like SSA, might prove interesting.
• The blocks were drilled into cores at dierent maturities, and it has not been looked
into what eect this has on the nal compressive strength. It should be examined.
• The hardening in these projects has been done in a climate-controlled room, but
there were still problems with samples drying out. It could be looked into if hard-
ening the samples under water would yield results that are more consistent.

90
Conclusion

Appendix B
EPD
This appendix contains
• Input: EPD of coal y ash from Emineral and EPD of Rapid cement from Aalborg
Portland. The data from these EPD's are used in the EPD-tool used in this project.
Only the relevant pages are shown.
• Output: EPD of all nine scenarios listed with the reference rst and then following
SSA.10 and onwards. These EPDs are not valid, because they have not been veried
by a third party.

==PAGE:137==
Water solubility
29
D.2
Water solubility
Purpose
This method is based on the description in [20] which determines the amount of water
soluble material, which is usually salt, in ash.
The procedure is performed on SSA
delivered directly from the production at Avedøre.
Equipment
Plastic bottle with lid, 1 L
Filter paper
Glass funnel
Measuring glass, approx. 1 L
Distilled water
Petri dish
Oven reaching 100 ◦C
Procedure
100 g. of dried SSA is mixed with 500 mL distilled water in the plastic bottle. The
bottle is shaken and sat on the table to settle. A lter paper is weighed and placed in
the funnel, which is placed in the measuring glass. After settlement of the ash, the top
liquid in the bottle is poured into the lter. Another 500 mL distilled water is added, the
bottle shaken and after settlement the top liquid is poured into the lter. This procedure
is repeated at least 3 times before the whole content is poured into the lter, making
sure that no ash is left in the bottle. The lter is left in the funnel over the night to dry.
Next day the lter and ash is placed in a petri dish and weighed before placed in an oven
over night at 100 ◦C. The following day the petri dish containing the lter with the ash
is weighed. The soluble material is calculated as a percentage of the dry mass of SSA.

==PAGE:139==
Loss on Ignition
31
D.3
Loss on Ignition
Purpose
This method is based on the method described in The Book[20] which determines the
loss on ignition for soil. In this case the method is used for SSA delivered directly from
the production at Avedøre. The organic material in substances such as soil and SSA is
more or less equivalent to the loss on ignition at 550 ◦C. If the substance contains heavy
metals these will, to a great extend, react with the organic material and be bound in
that fraction.
Equipment
Furnace reaching 550 ◦C.
Procedure
The furnace is heated to 550 ◦C and three crucibles are placed in the furnace for half an
hour. Afterwards the crucibles cool o in an desiccator and are weighed on an analytical
balance. It is important always to carry the crucibles with a tong, as ngers will leave
moist which will change the weight.
Each crucible is lled with approx. 2.5 g of SSA, and is placed in the furnace at 550 ◦C
for one hour. Afterwards the crucibles cool o in an desiccator, see gure D.1, and are
weighed on an analytical balance. The loss on ignition is calculated as a percentage of
the dry mass of SSA.

==PAGE:141==
Solubilisation - ICP
33
D.4
Solubilisation - ICP
Purpose
This method is based on Danish Standard 259, which determines the content of acid
soluble metals in soil. In this case the method is used for SSA taken directly from the
production at Avedøre.
Equipment and chemicals
Inductively Coupled Plasma - ICP
500 mL Nitric acid, HNO3, half-concentrated 1:1 with 500 mL distilled water.
Procedure
1.00 g of dry SSA is placed in a pyrex-glass with tap. 20 mL of HNO3 (1:1) is added with
a pipette and the bottle is closed thoroughly to avoid evaporation of the nitric acid. The
bottle is placed in the autoclave at 200 kPa and 120◦C for 30 min. Afterwards the bottle
is cooled to room temperature and the sample is ltered with suction through a 0,45 µm
lter in a chemical fume hood. After pouring the sample into a 100,00 mL volumetric
ask and adding distilled water to obtain 100 mL, and then pouring the sample into a
20 mL plastic vials, the sample is ready for ICP. The results given by ICP is measured
in mg/L. Formula D.1 is used to convert the result into mg/kg.
mg/kg = A · 0.100L · C
B
(D.1)
where:
A = The metal concentration of the sample [mg/L = ppm]
B = Ash [kg]
C = Dilution factor
